This bizarre little piece of Africa sits northwest of
Maunaloa Village. The 1,500-acre game preserve is home
to many imported African animals, from giraffes and
zebras to antelope and ostriches.

Ovhu

The most populated of the islands, O'ahu is the center
of human activity in Hawai'. It is the main arrival point
for anyone coming by plane (Honolulu Airport) or by
luxury boat.

While the population clusters mainly on the south
side of the island, the rest of the island is well inhabited.
This was the place where Kamehameha consolidated his
main empire, aftera bloody battle in which his army forced
the defending army overacliffto their deaths on the sharp
lava outcrops below. After this battle, only Kaua'i re-
mained unconquered.

Going from east to west around the island:

Oiamond Dead

The huge dormant volcano of Diamond Head (so-
called because early British mariners thought from afar
that the glistening crystals on its sides were diamonds)
dominates the eastern horizon of the island. The moun-
tain can be climbed, although it may take close to an hour,
and the view from the top is spectacular — a 360-degree
look at the island and ocean to the east. At its base is the
film studio where shows such as "Hawaii Five-O" and
"Magnum P.1

Waikiki

Waikiki is the hub of tourism on the islands and a
favorite spot for swimmers and surfers. Its beach line is

." were shot.

crowded with hotels and restaurants. Shops and stores of
all types huddle behind the hotels as close to the shore as
possible.

The Kapi‘olani Regional Park is home to some popu-
lar local attractions, such as the Honolulu Zoo and the
Waikiki Aquarium, where most forms of native Hawai'ian
wildlife can be found. The aquarium has many endangered
species, such as the monk seal, and is renowned for
breeding live coral in captivity.

Surfing is the sport of choice off the beaches, and the
area is perfect for it. Jet skis and other craft are not allowed
nearby, so the surfers have the waves to themselves. It was
on this beach that Duke Kahanamoku got famous, and
there isa section of beach named after him near the Hilton
and lagoon. A statue of him and his board stand on the
beach at Kuhio Beach Park.

The waves off Waikiki Beach (at Canoesand Queens)
are some of the most popular, although other sites down
the beach (westward) include Paradise and Number Threes.

Also on Waikiki Beach is the Royal Hawai'ian Hotel,
located in front of the sands where King Kamehameha
ordained the Malahiki games, dedicated to the god Lono.
The sacred altar of Lono was located in front of what isnow
the Sheraton-Waikiki Hotel.

Westward is the Fort DeRussey Beach, where Battery
Randolph is located. This early twentieth century muni-
tions dump was built too well; its impregnable walls proved
indestructible to the wrecking ball, and thus it still stands,
but now doubles as a museum.

The Ali Wai Yacht Harbor is always filled with the
pleasure ships of the rich and famous of Hawai'i and their
guests. It is connected to the Ali Wai Canal, a channel
that governs water run-off from the inland mountain
heights. The water can be quite brackish at times.

Watkiki Freehold

Inward toward the mountains, although still within
sight of Honolulu and Waikiki, is the freehold of
Queen Aeron's governor, Count Menhiron. It is lo-
cated in a private mansion, once the property of a
macadamia nut baron. It still appears as an affluent
private residence to all mortals, but to the fae, it is a
more wonderful palace of tropical royalty. The interior
is an eclectic mix of Polynesian artifacts and old-style
European decor (the governor is sidhe, after all).

Rumors of the opulence of Yrtalien's freehold
have recently reached him, and the governor has
begun to feel that he has been lax in his grandeur. In
an attempt to correct this he has set about improving
his home away from home, vowing that Kithain the
world over will come to envy his palace. He doesn't
realize yet that this is an insult to his queen, an
attempt to outdo his liege. When she finds out, she
will not be happy.

Fornow, itisstill a nice place to visit. Itis centrally
located and near to just about any Waikiki or Honolulu
location, so festivals and activities may be visited
without an uncomfortable journey. The governor has a
small fleet of fine cars that are at the disposal of his
valued guests or can be loaned with a chauffeur (for
commoners on noble business; they aren't trusted to go
off with the cars by themselves).

At any time of the day, local nobility may be
seen visiting, either to take tea or chat up politics
with the governor. Commoners are also welcome if
their business is not boring or tedious, and many

Chapzer Three: Geography


arrive to give the governor a glimpse of a new local
artist's work or to tell of some interesting and optimis-
tic news. Sometimes, these artists are invited to the
house, as the governor's mortal seeming is that of a
wealthy patron of the arts.

Less idyllic activities may also be pursued here,
since every court has its backstabbers. Spies for
Yrtalien's Shadow Court have recently been placed
close to the governor, and they will be wary of any
threat to Yrtalien or the Unseelie fae of Hilo. Certain
local fae (not natives, but early immigrants) resent
the return of the nobility and may secretly try to
recruit the discontented to their side. They too have
their spies in court.

Changeling Rocet

On Gray's Beach is the Halekulani Hotel, run by
an entrepreneurial boggan. The original location was
a hut owned by a man who would let the fishermen.
beach their boats here — the only place common folk
were allowed to bring their boats then, for the rich
had taken over the rest of the beach. The grateful
fishermen named the place the "House Befitting
Heaven" (Halekulani). Later, the hotel was built on
the land and became the setting for a Charlie Chan
mystery novel, The House Without a Key (referring to
a time when Hawai'ians did not lock their doors).

The hotel allows human guests, all unaware of the
special nature of the hotel's owners. The best rooms,
however, are reserved for Kithain. On nights of special
feasts or celebrations, parts of the hotel are shut off from
the view of mortals ("reserved for special parties").

The staff of the hotel, all Kithain or enchanted
mortals, ensure that a vacation atmosphere is always
maintained. The facade of a fantasy land of stress
relief is what attracts tourists to the island in the first
place, so most are only too willing to accept the
hotel's laid-back hospitality and eagerness to make
their vacations the most relaxing possible.

Downtown Honolulu is six miles west of Waikiki.
High-rise towers and business centers take the forefront
here, rather than tourist hotels.

The streets of Honolulu were once rowdy and wild,
where whalers and other mariners ran drunk and free.
Many saloons and brothels sprang up to service their
needs. But the missionaries arrived to spoil the fun. One
church was even built between two popular bars.

One bar, the Oahu Follies, was host to Preacher
Bingham's loud sermons on the street outside, directed at
the sinners within. One of those "sinners" may well have
been Herman Melville, on leave from his ship, swigging
back some relief and perhaps dreaming of the novel he
would one day write about whaling. Anyone familiar with
Moby Dick may recognize the Preacher.

Chinatown is just west of downtown, and is, like
Chinatown in San Francisco, a miniature Hong Kong,
where Chinese stores and restaurants offer a plethora of
imported Asian goods.

‘lolani Palace was once the home of the islands'
royalty and is now a restored museum reflecting that time.
Tours are available to see the finely furnished and opulent
palace.

Across from the judiciary building downtown is a
statue of Kamehameha the Great. Leis are thrown on it
every July 11 (Kamehameha Day).

South of downtown on the piers is the Maritime
Museum, once the Kalakaua Boat House where King
Kalakaua traded stories with his friend, Robert Louis
Stevenson. The museum includes a whale skeleton, arti-
facts from Polynesian cultures and the modern replica of
an ancient seafaring canoe.

North of the city is a campus of the University of
Hawai'i, shared with the East-West Center. Also north-
ward is the Tenrikyo Mission, a Japanese temple moved
here from Japan, and Queen Emma's Summer Palace, the
summer home of Hawai'ian royalty now refurbished for
the public.

In Punchbowl Crater is the National Memorial Cem-
etery of the Pacific, where many World War I Hawai'ian
servicemen are buried. There is also a memorial to Ellison
Onizuka, the Hawai'ian astronaut who was lost on the
shuttle Challenger.

East of the Punchbowl is the Hawai'ian Academy of
the Arts, which holds probably the most eclectic collec
tion on the island, from native crafts, to Asian art and the
works of many European masters.


Dear! Hardor

This famous military base was the target of the Japa-
nese surprise attack on December 7, 1941, which initiated
the United States' involvement in the war. The U.S.S.
Arizona Memorial commemorates the servicemen lost in
the war, and is a very modern looking monument, built on
topof the sunken battle cruiser from which it takes itsname.

Leeward

The west coast of the island (leeward side) is the least
developed part. It is home to many rural residents, who
have resisted the lure of big shopping malls (these can
certainly be found elsewhere on the island).

Makaha Beach is host to the February Buffalo Big
Board Surfing Contest. Makaha means "savage," which is
something the surf can certainly be. But the locals can also
be somewhat mean to outsiders who come to invade their
waves.

Kahokuwielowelo Neiau

This ancient temple wasa gathering place for kahunas
to gaze at the stars. The site was named aftera comet. (The
Menehune surreptitiously visit this place for prophecy; on
nights of spectacular celestial activity, such as meteor
showers, all uses of Soothsay receive one extra success.)

Waimea Bay

During winter months, this is a world nexus for

surfing. The Triple Crown is held here in November and
December. The waves of the north coast can tower as high
as 40 feet.

QDalaekahana Stace Dark

This quiet reserve is not incredibly popular with
tourists and so seems like an escape from the bustle of the
rest of the island. South of this place is a local campus of
Brigham Young University and the Polynesian Cultural
Center.

Ulpo Reiau

South of Kailua town is the Ulipo He'iau. It isa large,
well-preserved platform said to have been built (in one
night, of course) by the Menehune.

Ratona Cove

This small beach is where the famous love scene in
"From Here to Eternity" was filmed. Visitors still drop by
to see it. Nearby is the Halona Blowhole, a mini-geyser
phenomenon, where the sea water is forced up a tall lava
channel, spouting out from the top with great force.


This island, one of the oldest in the chain, is truly
formed by magic, for many of the ancient structures on the
island are said to have been built by Menehune. The
natural beauty of the island awes many visitors. Mere
words cannot fully describe the grandeur of the place, from
the central mountain Wai'ale'ale down to the rich green
jungles and the rift canyon of Waimea.

Beginning in the south and heading west from there:

Lihue andDo1pu

The capital of the island was founded by sugar barons,
for Kauai's main crop has always been sugar. Tourism is
now spreading to the isle as sugar prices fall, threatening
the stable economy and lifestyles of the residents. Sugar
fields fill the vicinity of Lihu'e.

The Kaua'i Museum is full of native artifacts and
other items from the island's rich history, including a map
suggesting the layout of native villages at the time of
Captain Cook's arrival.

The Menehune Fish Pond is still home to fish to this
day. This structure is said to have been on the island before
the Tahitians arrived, and is one of the many places built
by the Menehune crafters.

South of the capital is the old Koloa Sugar Mill, where
sugar cane is still ground. Nearby are two beautiful gar-
dens, the National Botanical Gardens and Lawai Kai. The
latter has many unique environments and sculpted land-
scapes, along with a wide variety of flora.

Still further south is Poipu, a growing center for
tourism on the island, where a Hyatt Regency hotel
attracts many visitors to the local beaches. The waves are
steady and safe, good for beginning surfers, and a nearby
beach is considered perfect for children.

Spouting Hole is another of Hawai'i's blowholes and
is an interesting attraction when it spouts water. The area
was devastated in 1992 by Hurricane Iniki, and some of the
local buildings have not yet been rebuilt.

Slane Toum, Canyon
and Kiokee

West of Poipu is Waimea Town, a coastal village
where Captain Cook first set anchor among the islands.
Every February, the town has a Captain Cook carnival
with games and rides.

The ruins of Fort Elizabeth are nearby, the result of a
Russian scheme in the 1800s to place a base on the islands.
But the scheme, by Anton Schaeffer, was not approved by
the tsar, and the schemer was eventually run off the island.

44 Immorcal Eyes: Shadows on the hu

Local (enehune

Menehune still come to fish from their sacred pond,
although these days they come in the guise of human
fishermen, since they are now changelings, and wear
mortal flesh just as their European cousins. It is said that
fish caught from this pond are especially pure and good for
ceremonial lu'aus.

Some Menehune crafters work at the sugar mill
and in the fields. They love the sweet sugar and the
agrarian lifestyle, fearing for the future of this last
bastion of the past as more and more tourists come
every year.

The Spouting Hole is said to hold trapped mo'o, or
lizard spirit, who was caught in its strong current on his
way back from Ni'ihau while mourning his lost sister. The
moaning from the hole is said to be his cries of anguish.

The most famous local attraction, besides the entry to
the trail into Waimea Canyon, is the Menehune Ditch, an
agricultural ditch built centuries ago with engineering
techniques unknown to the Hawai'ians. Archaeologists
are baffled by the site, said to be build by Menehune.

North lies the Waimea Canyon, a magnificent gorge
sometimes called the "Grand Canyon of the Pacific." Its
intense greenery, mist and sunlight provide an array of
brilliant colors, often with rainbow accompaniment. Few
witness it without gasping.

Still further north is the Koke'e State Park, refuge of
Kaua'i's wildlife, including many endangered species. A natu-
ral history museum is present, along with camping facilities.

Hikers can move further inland to the Alaka'i wet-
lands, a large swamp where some of the truly endangered
wildlife of the island still live. The chilly, misty swamp can
be a dangerous place, with illusory depths that can drown
the unwary traveler. Mists and fog can swirl in, obscuring
landmarks and causing people to quickly lose their way.

CDounz Waraleale

The remains of the extinct volcano which once birthed
Kaua'i take up a large part of the central core of the island.
The top of the mountain is hidden in rain clouds, and the area
is perhaps the wettest place in the world, receiving more
annual rainfall than anywhere else. Its sunless slopes host
small trees that hug to the side of the tall, wet monolith.

Nearby is Kalalau Lookout, a popular scenic spot with
a view of the northern Na Pali Coast. The hiking trails
provide many beautiful vistas, places where the quality of
light changes dramatically as the day progresses.

UCKGH

CDenehune Nome

The Menehune are brought from the mountains of
Pw'u-ka-pele and the sound ("hum") of their voices gives
ise to the saying, "Wawa ka Menehune i Pu'ukapele ma
Kaua'i, puoho ka manu o ka loko 0 Kawainui ma
Koolauloa, Oahu" (The noise of the Menehune at
Pu'ukapele on Kaua'i startles the birds on the fishpond of
Kawainui at Koolauloa, Oahu)

— Martha Beckwith, Hawai'ian Mythology

Moe'uhane, the village of Dream, rests at the foot
of Mount Wai'ale'ale, past the Alaka'i wetlands, which
act as a guard against wandering hikers. Menehune
sentries hide in the swamp, calling on their friends, the
fog, trees and waters to turn hikers away through misdi-
rection. If any get past the swamps, the Menehune will
follow them to ensure they don't get too close to the
hidden village. Ifnothing else works, they will approach
the hikers dressed as park rangers or traditional natives
andask the hikers to leave, givingas an excuse excessive
flooding from the mountain.

The village itself is near to a lagoon, a beautiful, still
grotto fed by a tumbling waterfall. Also nearby are the

fields of taro, a purple, potato-shaped plant that the

Menehune tend as their main food source and from
which they make poi, a mushy, nutritious staple. The
Menehune used to grow massive, magical crops near the
famous Menehune Ditch, but they have forsaken their
old site due to tourism and the Banality that comes with
the visitors.

The village is an old-style Hawaiian homestead, with
grass huts and a traditional he'iau, guarded by carved idols
meant to scare away evil spirits (they do). The Menehune
gather flowers from the nearby parks and return to the
village to make beautiful leis, which they wear for their
lwaus and other spiritual ceremonies.

On very rare occasions, the Menehune will bring a
human to the village to participate in ceremonies with
them. This is always a native Hawai'ian who is aware of
the traditional ways, usually a musician or crafter. This
fellow is enchanted and bedazzled and rarely remembers
the full details of his trip to fairyland. He or she usually
wakes up the next morning ona beach. Regardless of how
he searches for his lost fairyland, he is rarely allowed to
find it again. However, his joyful night instills in him a
newfound respect for traditional ways and inspires his art
more, providing much Glamour for the Menehune in the
audience at his next show.


Pacific CDissile Range

Back to the coast and continuing west while bending
north is the Pacific Missile Range, a Navy facility forthe
monitoring of sea activity. The sophisticated facility can
monitor ships up to a 1,000 miles away. The beautiful
beaches are open to the public, and there isa state park
nearby thatis good for solitude and campingunderthe stars.

NaDah Coase

This ruggedly beautiful coast is one of the most
intense sites on the islands. Mile after mile of huge cliffs
interspersed with white beaches. The beaches are inacces-
sible from the land, so boats must be used. The beautiful
interior was once home to natives who lived on taro
cultivation. Menehune structures and temples remain and
were popular with hippie immigrants in the 1960s, al-
though the land is uninhabited now.

Hana Scace Dark

Ruins of very ancient Hawai'ian villages can be found
here, along with Ke Ahu o Laka, a terrace for the perfor-
mance of sacred hula dances. Kaulaupaolau He'iau is
nearby, a temple dedicated to Laka, the goddess of the

hula. Maniniholo Dry Cave is one of many local caves said

to have been dug by Pele.

Ranater

This bay and plateau is home to the largest taro
patches in Hawai'i, producing poi for all the islands.
Princeville is a huge development complex, complete
with a Sheraton hotel, condos, golf courses, shopping
center and a small airport.

Hanalei Village is home to many artists and crafters
and their work can be found in various shops along these
quaint streets. A wide variety of mediums can be found,
from painting to glasswork and basketry to jewelry, cloth-
ing and even photography.

Pohahu Reiau

A temple dedicated to Poliahu, a snow goddess,
remains here, built in a Tahitian style. This was once a
place of human sacrifice. Nearby is the Bell Stone, which
was struck to send out a bell-like ring at the birth of new
royalty. Wailua Falls often tosses fish into the air as the
water plunges to a pond below.

The Sleeping Gianc

This mountain range was once a living giant who
laid here to rest after a feast. Menehune tried to wake
himup by throwing rocksat him, which he didnotlike
atall, so he ate the Menehune and went back to sleep,
his body slowly turning to rock as the years passed. He
is obviously now the dream of an earlier time, and no
one knows why he still exists.

Sometimes, on nights of rare Glamour, the giant's
sleeping body can be seen among (part of?) the moun-
tains. But he does not want to be awakened. If someone
issuccessful at this task, the giant will try to eat them, and
then roll over again to continue its centuries-long nap.
No one knows why he sleeps so long, but they wonder
what would happen if he ever got up and walked away.

It is said that this chimera could answer many

Nithau

The last of the large islands, Niihau is privately
owned by the Robinson Niviahu Ranch. Visitors can fly
over in helicopters, but may only land to the north and
south of the island. Tourism does not come to Ni'ihau.
The island is in the past in many ways; absent are many
modern conveniences. There is hardly any electricity, and
there are no telephones, no guns allowed, and no crime.

The residents, mainly native Hawai'ians, are ranch
hands, herding the vast flocks of sheep. They also make
native crafts thatare sold elsewhere. The island is perfectly
cutoff from the worst of modern influences. The Menehune
often visit, and some live here as ranch hands.


The Gar Never Ended

The island is haunted by the wraith of a Japanese
fighter pilot who crash-landed here during the attack
on Pearl Harbor. After struggling with the peaceful

sand shooting one of them five times, his head
was bashed into a rock. But this did not kill him. He
committed hari kari, ritual suicide, and now roams
restlessly as a wraith.

As much of a cliche as this sounds, the war is not
over for him. He did not get the respect he deserved as a
warrior and feels he died an ignoble death, humiliated by
aprimitive Hawai'ian who received a Medal of Honor for
winning the "Battle of Ni'ihau," the first U.S. victory
over Japan in the war.

Until he can prove his valor and readiness to die
for an Imperialistic Japanese cause, he will continue
to roam the Shadowlands, returning here occasion-
ally to scare the locals.

For the most part, the string of atolls stretching for
hundreds of miles through the Pacific Ocean are barren of
animal life, e] e occasional sea-bird seeking a
temporary roost. None of the atolls is inhabited by humans.

These tiny islands are the merest tips of larger rock
formations rising from the sea bed. Once active volcanoes,
now extinct for centuries, erosion has taken the bulk of
their masses back into the sea, leaving the barest tips
exposed to the winds.

Rokea Rancing Grounds

The waters off some of these atolls are used by

Rokea weresharks as breeding grounds (they breed
with sharks). During breeding times, they are intensely
protective of their territory and will take down any
ship that comes near. These times vary with ocean
currents, and it is hard to predict them.

Unknown to anyone but the Rokea, some of the
seamounts (undersea atolls) surrounding the isles are
magical. One of them, Loihi seamount, is actually a
site of magical power. The Rokea resent the recent
scientific curiosity concerning Loihi, which has been
discovered to bea growing volcano. Loihi has erupted
before, spreading lava across the ocean bottom. The
Rokea have begun a campaign to discourage undersea
exploration around Loihi, vowing to destroy undersea
cameras and even divers if they come too close.


The abundance and variety of life on Hawai'i is
astonishing. The ingenious adaptations of some flora and
fauna to their environment is unique, such as the i'iwi,
whose bill has adapted perfectly to drinking nectar from
the native long-throated lobelias.

But these forms are endangered, and many have
already become extinct. Nowhere else in the United
States are there so many endangered species. The islands'
native species have simply not had the time to evolve to
resist the predation of foreign species.

Realization of the crisis has finally come about of late,
but it may be too late for many species. The uphill battle to
protect wildlife reserves from rampant development has
angered many people against conservationists, who are
sometimes seenas preventing residents from makingmoney.
Tourism endangers the wildlife, but it also protects it in
cases in which it is recognized that tourism would benefit
more from a varied and lively, beautiful ecosystem than
from a few more hotels and condos to ruin the skyline.

Hora

While many plants in Hawai'i were introduced by the
Polynesian immigrants, such as the taro plant, others grew
and evolved here on their own, free from predation.

Among the plants of the island are the ‘ahinahina
(Silversword), which was once abundant but is now rare.
The seven foot, swordlike flower bears many yellow flower
heads. This plant serves as a lesson in the ecological
connectedness: goats were once blamed for its slow disap-
pearance, but it was discovered that Argentine ants were
preying on the native yellow-faced bee, who were neces-
sary to pollinate the silversword. With the decline of the
bee, the Silversword soon followed, and it was realized that
the goats were simply the most visible
plant.

scavengers of the

Mokihana berries grow only on Kaua'i. Their perfume
is greatly desired for the making of leis. The trees grow to
about 20 feet, with green leaves and berries.

The ‘ohi'alehua blossom is sacred. It grows on ‘ohi'a
trees, famed for their ability to sprout in lava landscapes.
The plant and blossom is thus favored by Pele. The
tenacious, sometimes bonsailike trees were once used for
canoes and temple idols. The blossom is used in leis for
hulas dedicated to Pele.

Many other flowers are preferred for making leis. The
yellow-gold ‘ilima flower is especially popular. Also good
are the orange kauna'oa, pink lokelani, green kukui and
even white pupu shells (from Ni'ihau).

Immorzal Cyes: Shadows on the hu

Birds arrived on the islands centuries ago either
through great endurance when blown off course in flight
or by clinging to driftwood and floating to the islands.
Only a few original species arrived, but these few blos-
somed into over 70 distinct species. A better example of
evolution from a single species is found no where else in
the world.

Hawai'i is home to the honeycreeper, unique to the
islands. The ‘apapane is the most common survivior, and
it is often camouflaged among the red blossoms of
the'ohi'alehua.

The ‘alala, or Hawaiian crow, is one of the rarest birds
in the world. Its population was vastly reduced by ranch-
ers, who shot them as scavengers. The birds' black feathers
are a symbol of royalty.

The brown ‘elapaio, or Flycatcher, was once the pa-

tron of canoe makers. Wherever it pecked, it warned the

carvers away from bad trees.

‘To is the Hawai'ian hawk. He is associ:
royalty and is considered an ‘aumakua (ancestor s
many.

The sociable nene is the Hawai'ian goose and state
bird. It has recently been brought back from near extinc-
tion by careful breeding, but still faces foreign predators in
the wild.

The ‘o'o was valued for its yellow feathers, and it is
said that over 80,000 of them went into King
Kamehameha's cloak. It is now believed extinct, although
some are rumored to live in the Alaka'i wetlands on
Kaua'i.

The pueo is another ancestorspirit. It is the Hawai'ian
owl, hunting for the rats that were accidentally introduced
by the early Tahitians and later whalers.

(Dammals

The monk seal is one of Hawai'i's most famous endan-
gered species. Brought back from the brink of extinction,
this kindly seal flops on Kaua'i's beaches and on atolls in
the sea.

The hoary bat was once the largest native predator on
the islands, having arrived from a long flight many centu-
ties ago. It has been joined by much larger mammals since.
It is mainly found on Hawai'i and Kaua'i

The pig came with the early Polynesians, but after
Cook brought and released European pigs, the interbreed-
ing produced something much like the European boar, and


it has been a danger to the islands' ecosystems ever since.
It tears up native plants and bears foreign plant seeds in its
fur, leaving them to sprout up like weeds wherever it goes.
Nonetheless, it is still revered by traditional Hawai'ians.

Kamapuaia's Gec (Dig Ged)

These monsters are magical boars said to be
created from the dreams of traditional Hawai'ian boar
hunters. A Pig Get is the ideal incarnate of a perfect
pig: fierce and ravaging and large enough to feed an
entire village in one lu'au. They can be found snuf-
fling around the deep woods of the islands (especially
Hawai'i and Mau'i) a little before and after pig hunts.

They are larger than the average pig and are
perfectly formed. It is said by the Menehune that
when killed and eaten, Kamapua'a's Get provides the
diners with prophetic visions concerning the local
environment and its fate.

© Prophecy —Anyone eating the roasted flesh of
a properly slain Pig Get (to improperly slay one is a

Polynesians also brought dogs with them, but used
them forfood rather than friendship. RobertLouis Stevenson
had roast dog with his friend, King Kalakaua. The islands
are rabies-free and quarantines keep them that way.

The Polynesian rat wreaks havoc on the environment
in much the same way the pig does. They arrived stowed
aboard canoes and spread throughout the islands since
then. Hawai'ians used them in games, racing them before
spearing them.

Goats were introduced onto the island and have
ravaged certain areas before coming under control. None-
theless, wild goats still roam the mountainous regions.

Mongooses were introduced to control the rats but
went after nene instead. They have become a problem that
is too wild to easily fix.

Other animals include a wide variety of insects (in-
cluding asnow-top dweller with a sort of antifre
built into its blood to survive the temperatures) and

defense

lizards, including the bizarre-looking Jackson chameleon,
brought from Africa, that competes for the insects with
birds. Mosquitoes have also arrived, and brought deadly
diseases to the birds, who have no defense against these
continental diseases.

Fash

A fisherman's paradise, many anglers make their
living by fishing from the islands or by hosting tourists on
their boats.

Of the varieties found are some reef fish (seen when.
snorkeling), such as the parrotfish, goatfish, scorpionfish,
moorish idol and others. Popular eating fish are mahi-
mahi, mackeral (akule), swordfish (a'u), shark (mano), red
snapper (onaga), and jack fish (ulua).

The prize catch for deep sea fishing is the marlin, the
most soughtafter catch by fishermen from the Kona Coast.

Tocems

The Menehune are friends with the spirits of
their natural environment. They ally with the spirits,
or totems, of many of the local plants, rocks and
animals. When in the presence of their chosen totem,
many of themcan "step sideways" into the spirit world.

Among the most popular totems are Mokihana,
Palm, Lava and even Shark. See the Appendix for
more information on these totems.

Officially, Hawai'i came under the rule of the King-
dom of Pacifica in 1969, when Queen Aeron sent a
governor to claim the region. This governor, Count
Menhiron, settled in O'ahu, avoiding the smaller, less-
populated islands. He has never paid any attention to the
Menehune, and the Menehune prefer to keep themselves
isolated from these strange fae with their odd customs.

Counc QDenhiron

Menhiron received his title in exchange for his move
to Hawai'i. He had been studying law at Berkeley and was

only peripherally involved in local Seelie politics when
Queen Aeron approached him. The thought of living in
ise, greatly untouched by the Banality of
appealed to him so much that he dropped

an island p:

out of school and left on a plane the next day.

Menhiron has enjoyed a life of leisure for nearly 20
years. On the winter end of wilder, he has managed to
remain blissfully unaware of the activities of both the
Unseelie in Hilo and the Menehune on Kaua'i. He wants
no trouble and no problems. Menhiron has led a peaceful
lifestyle for so long that he will not tolerate any disruptions
of it. He ignores the rumors and throws troublemakers
bearing bad news out of his freehold.

Count Menhiron makes regu
to Queen Aeron that include disheartening descriptions

compulsory reports

of the increasing tourist trade, the giant convention cen-
ters being raised along the beaches and Spring Break. He
never mentions anything about silly rumors regarding
Unseelie activity in Hilo.


On a high hill, overlooking O'ahu, sits Menhiron's
freehold. To the mortal eye, the freehold appears to be a
ramshackle warehouse once used by sugarcaners in the
area. To the fae, however, the building is a work of art. Set
back in a copse of coconut palms and sugar cane, the outer

ls of the two-story mansion are trellises covered with a
thick profusion of flowering vines. The roof, covered with
giant translucent leaves, allows gentle light but no rain to
filter in. On the interior, stone walls separate the rooms. A
waterfall trickles down one in the main room, emptying
intoa goldfish pond that empties into a narrow brook that
runs to the rear of the house and out into the garden.
cotic planes

In the garden, the rich scents of many

make the air heavy and sedative. Stone benches sit along
walkways that cut paths through the lush foliage. Menhiron
holds his parties and his court here. He relishes the tropical
beauty of his freehold, rarely leaving it to wander into the
city of O'ahu.

Nuaella(Netly Chicherspoon)

Nelly grew up in the streets of London. Her father
deserted her and her mother when she was only six years
old. Afterward, Nelly's mother tried to recapture her
youth, flitting from man to man in the hope of finding one
who would support her. Some of her lovers, she discovered
too late, were already married. Others took their own
frustrations out on her, beating her in front of Nelly.
Nelly's mother eventually turned to drugs and alcohol to
ease the pain of her loveless life. At last, she couldn't take
it any more. Nelly found her mother in the bathroom ina
pool of her own vomit, dead from an overdose of barbitu-
rates. At the age of 16, Nelly was alone with no means of
support. Rather than live ina foster home, Nelly ran away.

At first, Nelly did not fare so well. She ended up
prostituting herself in order to survive. Her youth and
beauty served her well. She found a position in a semi-
respectable cat house. She worked there for two years,
until one night, she kicked the wind out of a john who
wanted more than his money warranted. Upset and angry
that her employer would throw her out, Nelly put on her


best dress and hit the streets again, determined to find
herself a sugar daddy — that night or else...

Inaseedy London bar, Nelly worked the room. Asshe
got drunker and louder, her clothes strategically slipping
here and there, Nelly ended up in the lap of a gap-toothed,
round-bellied, belching welder from York. He groped and
she giggled; she asked for money and he dumped her on her
ass. Then fate smiled. A handsome, well-bred gentleman
helped her off the floor. He wiped the mascara tears from
her face and took her back to his hotel. He made her bathe
and dressed her in a flowing white peignoir. Then he
forced her to look, for the first time, at her faerie self, and
Nelly remembered. It felt like she was coming down from
a bad high.

Once Nelly had recovered from her Chrysalis, she
could not have been more pleased. She spent hours gazing
in wonder at her own beauty. New plans began to form in
her head. Surely, with this, she thought, I won't have any
trouble finding someone to care for me. She began imme-
diately to insist everyone call her Nuaella, her faerie name.

Her benefactor, the sidhe who had found her, taught
her the fine manners of the courtesan, and she proved to
be a quick learner. For a while the two were lovers. Then
Nuaella discovered Hawai'i. She and her lover flew there
together to visit his friend, Menhiron. Nuaella never left.
She convinced her lover she wanted to stay in order to
learn about House Fiona from Menhiron. Her lover left
with no hard feelings. He even visits her from time to time.
In the meantime, Nuaella eats coconut and drinks mai-

tais with Menhiron in exotic luxury. She feeds his ego and

he takes care of her — quite well.

enhances her natural beauty. Tall and thin, her practiced
grace is a far step from the call girl swing she used to use.

Sammie

Sammie came to O'ahu three years ago. A monkey
pooka, Sammie had a hard time adjusting to city life. He
has traveled around the world, rarely stopping for long in
any one place. Throughout his travels, Sammie acquired
several valuable mortal treasures that he used to set up a
savingsaccount forhimself. Although not wealthy, Sammie
earns enough in interest to keep himself in bananas and
nuts. He actually spends little, and his savings are growing.

Sammie has always found the occult arts fascinating.
While in New Orleans, he studied Tarot and palm reading
with a fortuneteller. He also traveled to Africa one sum-
mer to learn the more primitive practice of tribal witch
magic. He especially loved dancing around the bonfires.
Although Sammie has seen these magics and believes in
them, he isnot dedicated enough to practice them himself.

Before coming to Hawai'i, Sammie lived in San

Diego. The Banality of the city slowly eroded his faerie
nature. He slipped into the Mists while in his monkey form
and, contrary to the normal effect of the Mists, did not
revert to his mortal seeming. Instead, he began wandering
the streets of San Diego in his monkey form. This caused
quite an uproar. The local SPCA sent outa team of officers
to capture him. He led them on a merry chase through a
local mall before they caught him and sent him to the San

Diego Zoo. The week he spent there had a lasting impact

Immorcal Cyes: Shadows on che hun

on Sammie. From behind his bars, he watched as people
made faces, laughed and threw things at him. He felt lost,
confused and humiliated.

Fortunately for him, his friend George, a seagull
pooka, saw the story of Sammie's capture on the news. He
orchestrated Sammie's escape and reminded the pooka of
his faerie nature. Sammie has never forgotten the debt he
owes George.

Sammie found the zoo experience so traumatizing
that he decided upon his release to leave the city, never to
return. He went to his travel agent, searching for the
perfect location. He settled on Hawai'i and convinced
George to go with him.

Sammie and George wandered around the islands for
several months before discovering Menhiron's freehold.
The count welcomed Sammie and his friend, although he
would now love to find an excuse to send them away. He
abhors Sammie's tendency to swing from anything he can
find. The pooka has broken several of Menhiron's posses
sions and pieces of his furniture. However, Nuaella adores
Sammie, and so Menhiron puts up with him.

Sammie loves nothing more than to swing through
the rain forest, swim in the crystal-clear pools and climb
the rock formations of the islands. Unlike Menhiron and
Nuaella, Sammie does not restrict himself to the island of
O'ahu, but has explored all the others as well. He has met
the Menehune, and they have invited him to visit at any
time.

a pair of silk shorts in complicated patterns of greens and
reds and golds. During formal occasions, he adds a light-
weight, white turtleneck and a green embroidered vest —
maybe even sandals if Menhiron insists.

Nahome

Nahome runs a small tiki bar on the beach at O'ahu.
Its ocean side has a canopy wall that opens up. Customers
can sit and watch the sun set over the Pacific, sipping their
fruit drinks. An eshu grump, Nahome tired of adventuring
and decided to settle down. She is the real attraction at the
bar. Her stories entertain mortal and fae customers year

Immorcal Cyes: Shadows on che hun

after year. She named the bar "Fairytales" in honor of her
eventful past.

Nahome has traveled extensively throughout the
world. She rarely tells the same story to the same people
twice. In the evenings, when the sun has set and her
customers have relaxed, Nahome steps up onto the small
stage and tells a tale from one of her travels. Mortals enjoy
the fairy tales as the product of an active imagination.
Kithain, however, listen more intently, knowing the truth
behind her words.

The changelings of Count Menhiron's court often
visit Fairytales. Although Nahome does not consider
herself a member of the court, she has fostered friendships
with all who are. Especially with Nuaella, who listens with
rapt attention to her stories of adventure. Some of the
Unseelie Court also patronize the bar. Their numbers
have increased lately, and from time to time they cause
trouble for Nahome. For this reason, she has hired a wilder

Chog

Chog has worked as a bouncer at Nahome's bar,
Fairytales, fora little over a year. Before that, he had been
working as a grunt on a cargo ship running between

and the mainland. One night while having dinner
at Nahome's tiki bar, he broke up a tussle between two
Unseelie ruffians and she offered him the job. It was an
offer he couldn't refuse.

Quiet and reserved, Chog talks sparsely and never
about himself. He has led a solitary life, taking odd jobs
here and there, falling in and out of the Mists. Nahome's
kindness has warmed him to her, and he considers her his
only friend. He loathes the falsity and pretentiousness of
the other Seelie on the island. And he bears no good will
toward the rowdy Unseelie. He does his job, then goes

home at night to the little efficiency apartment above

Kiraven

A newcomer to O'ahu, Kiraven has been spotted a
couple of times in Fairytales. Her sluagh nature keeps her
in the dark corners of the bar, watching the other patrons
from beneath her long bangs. Menhiron has shown par-
ticularcuriosity and discomfortatherpresence. He theorizes
that she is a spy for Queen Aeron.

On the contrary, Kiraven came to Haw
asylum from the insanity of San Francisco. She actually
followed Sammie here, although she came several years
later. She knew Sammie when he lived in San Diego, and
although his exuberance bothered her, he was kind to her.

Kiraven grew up a misunderstood child. Her mother
died when she was still a toddler, and her father sexually,
physically and emotionally abused her. He never let her go
anywhere without him. He sequestered her in her room
without television or books. For Kiraven's budding genius,
it was a fate worse than death. When she withdrew into
herself, everyone blamed her. They said she needed psy-
chiatric help.

Kiraven discovered her faerie nature when she hit
puberty. Her menstrual cycle kicked in and she remem-
bered who she was, all in the same instant. The experience
traumatized her. She had no one to guide her, no one to

eeking

down her neck

Kiraven believed she was going mad. Completely
isolated from the other Kithain, she blamed her period for
the strange changes coming over her. She thought she was
dying. In a daze, she roamed the streets of San Diego,
sleeping in dumpsters at night and sitting in the park
during the day. By the end of the week, Kiraven had
slipped back into the Mists. She became a street person,
eating food out of restaurant garbage bins and hiding from
the cops. Exactly 28 days later, her cycle came again and
her Glamour rose, returning her memory. This convinced
Kiraven that the two were connected and that she was
being punished for all the things her father had done to
her. She found a church and knelt down to pray. She
prayed for guidance and for a relief from her terrible curse.

Kiraven left the church, walking slowly down the
front steps, her long brown hair hanging in her eyes. She
didn't see the car comingas she stepped out into the street.
Fortunately for her, Sammie had seen it. He grabbed her
and pulled her out of the way. Sammie took Kiraven to his
place, where he fed her and let her sleep in comfort. They
felt extremely uneasy in each other's presence and talked
little. Instinctively, however, Sammie knew what he had
to do. He called a sluagh acquaintance of his in San
Francisco. She came immediately to take Kiraven under
her wing.

Kiraven spent the next couple of years devouring
every book she could get her hands on. It didn't matter
what the book was about, Kiraven read it like a starving
child consuming her first meal.

Roleplaying Hints: Life has dealt you a raw deal and
you know it. You are and always have been completely
alone in the world. Those that have helped you did so out
of pity rather than true caring. You don't need anyone's
pity. You can make it on your own. You feel like you've
proven that. Your best companions are the books you read
and the stories you hear from other fae.

All you want out of people is to be left alone. You
don't need or want to communicate with anyone. You'd
rather just sit back and watch, living life vicariously
through others. It's safer that way.

LordOevin

At one time, Lord Devlin sat on the House of Com-
mons of High King David. He had worked his way up
through the Seelie Court of Peoria, hiding his Unseelie
nature in order to extract secrets from them. Eventually,

they asked him to represent them at the House of Com-
mons. For years, Devlin, in the guise of a Seelie sidhe,
listened to the plottings and admissions of the other
membersof the House, reporting them back tothe Shadow
Court and Glynnis in San Francisco. Then he was discov-
ered.

High King David reacted badly, appalled that an
Unseelie would emulate a Seelie in order to gain informa-
tion. The Unseelie had always been welcomed in the
House of Commons, if they came openly. The High King
banished Devlin from the meetings. His motley not only

Argo and CDauler

Rarely do twin brothers both have changeling souls,
but in the case of Argo and Mauler, it happened. Their
bond is extremely strong because of it. They go everywhere
together. Born in the ghetto of New York, they ran the
streets for years, selling drugs and working as muscle for the
mob.

Their Chrys:
began when Mauler fell in love with a young woman
named Angie Moccioli. Unknown to Mauler, so did Argo.
A satyr, Angie awakened the twins to their faerie natures.

is came upon themas quite a surprise. It

For weeks, neither knew the other was seeing the same
woman. In their small minds, they thought it was merely
a fortunate coincidence that they should both discover a
gorgeous young satyr and their faerie natures at the same
time. Angie played one brother off the other until finally
they discovered the truth and came to blows, nearly killing
each other.

In the middle of that fateful fight, Argo and Mauler
stopped. They looked longand hard at one another. Then,
arm in arm, they went home and vowed to stay away from
Angie.


Within the year, they had affronted their boss and
acquired the sudden need to leave New York. They booked
a flight for what they thought was Hanover, but instead
ended up in Hawai'i. (They never were terribly bright.)
And now they work for Yrtalien.

Despite their physical similarities, Argo and Mauler

have distinct personalities. Argo, like his mother, loves
bright colors. He wears comfortable sweatpants and T-
shirts. Bandannas of every color are wrapped around his
ankles, his wrists and his head. His steel-lined boots were
bought from the dock worker's outlet, and he painted his
name in glow-in-the-dark green on the toes. Around his
neck, Argo sports a multicolored bead necklace with a
peace sign hanging down in the center, and in one ear he
wears a tiny Gemini symbol.

Mauler tends toward more traditional clothing, like
his father. He wears blue jeans, when he can find a pair
large enough to fit his body, and plain white T-shirts.
Chains and safety pins, attached to his black leather
jacket, jangle softly when he moves. On the back, in
running red letters, are the words, "Live and Let Die."
(Mauler loves old Bond flicks.) To top itall off, Mauler has
a ring in his nose and one at the end of his eyebrow.

In their troll seemings, Argo and Mauler do not
appear as identical as they do when in their mortal guises.
Nevertheless, they are still two large, ugly trolls who rarely

Lady Siva

Born to an affluent East Coast family, Lady Siva has
never wanted for anything. She spent her youth getting
kicked out of boarding schools for "improper" behavior,
usually involving boys or alcohol. Her parents paid her
way into and out of one finishing school after the other.
When old enough, Siva quit school entirely and became
thediva ofthe Club Med social crowd. She figured she may
as well have fun until her beauty started to fade, at which
time she planned to finda wealthy husband with whom to
settle down.

Siva discovered her faerie seeming on the eve of her
2st birthday. It came as quite a shock to her when, after
aparticularly acrobatic encounter witha young Harvardite,
she looked at herself in the mirror only to discover she was
a satyr. In that instant, she rediscovered her faerie soul.
Siva's next few hours were not pretty. The young man she
had been with fled in terror. He thoughtshe was dying, All
alone, Siva survived her Chrysalis.

Soon after, Siva moved to Hawai'i with a rich busi-
nessman more than twice her age. For the first time, she
met others like herself, the Unseelie of Hilo. Siva rejoiced
in her newfound freedom and friends.

When Yrtalien came to Hilo, Siva found herself
immediately attracted to his good looks and charm. She
decided to insinuate herself into his court and succeeded
quite well. Yrtalien made her a noble within the first few
moments of meeting her. Now Lady Siva, a commoner,
holds one of the few positions of nobility in Yrtalien's
Shadow Court.

Gamine Larouche

Born and raised in Hawai'i, the daughter of a succes

ful French hotel owner, Gamine has a rebellious streak a
mile wide. Her parents, her father in particular, were
always brutally strict. The stricter they were, the more
rebellious she became, the more strict they became, and so
on. In high school, Gamine dabbled in a multitude of
debaucheries in an attempt to create the illusion that she
had some control over her life. She tried every drug she
could get her hands on and entered into every possible
sexual situation she could imagine. When she discovered
sado-masochism, heavy on the "sado," she found the
empowermentshe had been seeking. Buteven that seemed
temporary and superficial; her mother and father still
controlled her.

When Gamine met Siva, she was overwhelmed by
the satyr's charm and sexuality. They became lovers, and
Siva taught her a means of empowerment more enriching
than any whips-and-chains encounter could ever be. Ga-
mine soaked in Siva's Glamour, at last remembering her
true nature. This alone gave her what she needed to break
free of the suffocating ties with her parents.

CDenehune

The hills look over on the South,

And southward dreams the sea;

And, with the sea-breeze hand in hand,
Came innocence and she.

— Francis Thompson, "Daisy"

The Menehune have occupied the island of Kaua'i in
Hawai'i since ancient times. Their own elders believe they
are the oldest of the fae. A tribal society, isolated from the
Banality of modern civilization, they have successfully
guarded many of their customs and much of their inno-
cence. The Menehune have their own word for Glamour
—they call it mana and they pull it from nature, the
indigenous rain forest surrounding them, rather than from
the dreams of mortals.

The Menehune have specific laws and traditions which
dictate their daily duties. Each individual hasa position and
a responsibility within the hierarchy of the tribe, like a cog
inamachine. They all work together under the direction of
the chief to ensure the continued safety and productivity of
the community. For more information on this exotic race of
changelings, see "Menchune," in the Appendix.

Certain abilities are available only to them because of
their traditions and lifestyle. Furthermore, some of the
Talent
only within their own environment. These ha'

ills and Knowledges of the Menehune apply

indicated in the character statistics below by an aster
behind the value. If the value listed is split, then the
second value applies to the use of the ability versus

outsiders. Only one value indicates that the character does
not have the ability at all when dealing with situations

beyond the normal experience of the Menehune.

Chief COakami

Chief Makani rules with a commanding, yet gentle
and diplomatic hand. He takes his responsibilities as the
village's chief seriously. According to Menehune tradi-
tion, the chief serves not only as the leader, but also as the
guardian of the village's mana. As a result, the chief has
greater access to mana than his people. Other traditions
abound concerning the treatment of the chief. These have
been passed down through many generations. For ex-
ample, no Menehune may speak in the presence of the
chief unless given permission to do so. Furthermore, tribal
taboos dictate that no one must ever allow her shadow to
fall upon the chief. The Menehune respect and honor

in hula dances from time to time. His soft heart and love

astrong relationship between him andhis followe

Makani lost his wife soon after the birth of their s
daughter. He entered intoa period of mourning that lasted
a year and avday following:the tragic accident. He has
raised his daughters as he knows his wife would have
wanted, keeping her memory alive with them. As per
Menehune custom, Chief Makani never took another
wife. His loneliness has been the greatest pain in his life.

Image: Chief Makani's dark hair has only just begun
to show signs of graying. His mature features give him an
air of wisdom and strength. His eyes shine with bubbling

nce and curio:

Chief Makani wears a cloak made of feathers from
various local birds such as the bird-of-paradise and the
peacock. Lined with hardened reeds, the cloak not only
glorifies his station, but also protects him. The tradition of
this cloak, called the Mantle of Birds, has passed from chief
to chief over the ages. The elders believe that the ali'i nui,
the Great Chief, wore one when she met with the leader
of the original Menehune, the builders, in ancient times.
When a chief dies, his people bury him in his Mantle of
Birds. The whole tribe then works together to make anew
one for the old chief's replacement.

Kanam

A prin among the Menehune and the eldest
daughter of Chief Makani, Kanani holds a special position
in her village. Someday, when her father dies, she will step
into the role of chief. Kanani began her preparations at an
early age. The Menehune believe that only a lifetime of
training can prepare one to be chief.

Kanani has studied the diplomatic and judicial re-
sponsibilities of a chief. She has learned to run with the
warriors and shoot with the hunters. She has crafted
beautiful leis from the island's blossoms and shells. She has
spent long hours with the kahunas, memorizing Menehune
history and learning the ways of the spirit world. And,
most importantly, she has studied mana, its uses, its nature
and its value. Although nowhere near as wise or knowl-
edgeable as her father, Kanani has reached a point in her


preparation where she would be accepted as chief if her
father were to die. The villagers now call her ali'iki or little
chief. This places her one step above the other ali'i of the
village. Kanani adores her father. She would do anything
for him and for the other Menehune, her sense of duty
outweighing any personal needs or desires she might have.
Raised in a protected environment amidst the beauty of
the Kaua'i rain forest, with its magnificent waterfalls and
beaches, Kanani embodies the typical Menehune inno-
cence. However, she has experienced the dangers of the
mortal world, seen its extravagances and its wastefulness.
Although Kanani does not understand the evils she has

she knows enough to distrust strangers. Their odd
ways frishvervand shock her.

Kanani's time in Hilo had an adverse affect on her
health and hermana. Livingamidst the Banality of the city
drained her mana, and the horrible events she witnessed
affected her peace of mind. Although she has regained
some of her vigor, she fears it will never return to its full
glory.

Ramaia

The position of kahuna carries with it a great deal of
honor. The Menehune consider it a sacred responsibility.
The kahunas each havea specific area in which they special-
ize. Because so much knowledge must be remembered, no
one kahuna can do it all. Although Hamaia knows the spirit
world, he specializes in the preservation of Menehune
history. He isa kahuna ka'i. The Menehune believe their lore


tobe the literal soul of their kith. They feel that losing these
chronicles would permanently wound them.

Hamaia has studied Menehune lore his entire life.
The rich history of the Menehune requires a lifetime of
preparation before one can consider himself completely
versed in it. Nearly every day throughout his life, Hamaia
has gone to the kahuna's hale, along with one or two
others, to learn about the traditions, arcana and history of
the Menehune. All of them prepared for the position that
eventually only one of them could hold.

The Menehune believe so strongly in the preserva-
tion of their lore that they take no risks in the preparation
of a kahuna ka'. Keiki iki spotted by the hoani'i as destined
to become kahuna ka'i spend their entire lives studying the
lore. When the kahuna ka'i dies, all trained kanaka must
compete to decide who will take her place. Traditionally,
keiki iki do not enter this competition.

The lore contest occurs overa three-day period and is
accompanied by feasting and revelry. All the Menehune
come out for the Lu'au Kahuna Ka'i in their finest clothes
and adornments. The lu'au serves not only as a means of
choosing the new kahuna ka'i but also as a wake for the
deceased. The celebration, with hula dancing, abundant
xhibitions of flame juggling and wrestling, lasts
three days and nights. No one but the contestants may
sleep, for to do so would show disrespect for the years of
work the students endured in preparation for this contest.

food,

The test requires that the contestants tell tales
requested by the chief. If the contestant's delivery has
missing elements or does not do what it is intended to do,
the student is eliminated. Thus, the contest continues
until only one remains, the new kahuna ka'i. The new
kahuna ka'i then becomes the focus of the lu au. Even the
losers celebrate, since no shame comes from losing. They
will still hold positions of respect and honor. They will
continue to study, knowing someday they may get another
chance. They also become the teachers, along with the
kahuna ka'i of the keiki iki who will eventually replace them.

Hamaia has been kahuna ka'i for many years. He has
earned a reputation as one of the greatest in the history of
the Menehune. His tellings evoke strong emotions.

Kenu

At the age of five, Kenu looked up at the hoani'i, then
Lulani's mother, and she pronounced that he would be-
come a great warrior, kokua. His grandfather and father
had both followed the path of the kahuna and they felt
some disappointment when Kenu did not follow them.
The word of the hoani'i, however, can never be doubted.
From that day forward, Kenu embraced the life of a kokua.

Kenu fights courageously in the warrior contests, kulai
umauma, of the Menehune and can throw a spear farther
and straighter than any of the other warriors. He has
proven his skill on many occasions and has even had
several lu'aus in his honor. All young warriors undertake

il AMK

adream quest when they leave their keiki iki selves behind.
Kenu's dream quest left a lasting impression on him.
Following tradition, he trekked into the rain forest to find
the "place that called to him."

After many hours, Kenu finally came to a clearing
where bougainvillia vines covered the ground and climbed
the trees, blossoms filling the air with their lush scent. To
one side, a waterfall poured over smooth rocks into a
glistening pool. Sunlight, filtering through the tops of the

16 Immorcal Cyes: Shadows on che lon

banyan and flame trees, created rainbows in the mist
coming off the waterfall. Kenu knew he had found his
"place."

For days, Kenu fasted and meditated there. He swam
in the pool and soaked in the mana of the grotto. On the
third day, Kenu slipped into the Dreaming. A giant bird-
of-paradise flew down to him, a kamano (salmon) in its
beak. The bird dropped the fish at Kenu's feet, then
stooped to drink in the pool. When it turned back around,
the bird had become a small changeling with reddish skin
and bulging eyes. The strange being smiled and said,
"Hauoli la hanau." Then it scampered away over the rocks
and quickly out of sight. Kenu awoke with a start, his
childling days behind him. In front of him lay the kamano.

As he contemplated his dream, he remembered a
story his grandfather had told him about a faerie with
reddish skin and enlarged eyes, one of the original
Menehune, the builders. He smiled. "Hauoli la hanau" is
the Hawai'ian equivalent of "Happy Birthday." Kenu had
become a kanaka. He picked up the fish and ended his
fasting. The fish became Kenu's spirit animal, and Kenu
soon found that his dream quest gift allowed him to swim
as no other Menehune warrior could.

Image: Kenu's tall, lean body moves like that of a
panther. He glides across the ground, muscles relaxed, yet

Whether dancing or hunting, his complete attention
remains fixed on the moment. Kenu speaks only when

convey pleasure or displeasure. Full and proud, his mouth
can be equally expressive. The black curls of his hair cling
tightly to the crown of hishead. Hissmooth, high forehead
bears no wrinkles or worry lines.

Asa kokua, Kenu wears only a cloth wrap around his
lower torso. Long and narrow, it goes around his hips, then
down through his legs and up again to tuck in at his waist.
Similar to a baby's diaper, it allows him freedom of move-
ment while preserving his modesty.

Roleplaying Hints: You run, jump and swim. You eat
heartily and sleep lightly. You throw your spear long and

are proud and strong. You are happy. You are a kokua. A

speak little, standing up tall and crossing your arms toshow
that you are a great warrior. You keep your eyes hard upon
their faces. They must not see any weakness in you. You
might have to kill them — if the chief wishes it.

to be yours once you have proven yourself.

Doki

Sixteen-year-old Poki dreams of seeing the world. He
feels the confinement of the village like the walls of a
prison cell. Poki loves his family and friends, but he has
seen glimpses of the outside world and wants to explore it.

Poki sits atop the highest cliff in Kaua'i, watching the
giant cruise ships and sailing boats go by. He sees the people,
tiny dots on their ships, and wonders what they are like.
Whenever malihini, strangers, come to the village, Poki's
parents forbid him from going near them. They sense his
unrest and unnatural curiosity toward the outside world.

The hoani'i, mother-hostess, announced Poki's place
in the Menehune. He is a fire-dancer. For years he has
trained in the ways of fire. He has learned the dances and
can juggle flaming batons, but his heart isn't in it. Time and
again, Chief Makani and his parents have explained to him
the importance of the fire-dancers. They honor the ancient
gods, especially Pele, the goddess of fire. Also, the fire-
dancers watch over the village fires, making sure they never
go out and that the cooking pits stay well-maintained. Poki
performs his duties, all the while wishing for the day when
he will leave the village.

Poki watched the strangers come and go. He saw their
boats travel away from the island, a sinking feeling in his
stomach. One of them, however, left something behind: a
small chimeric creature. A combination of unicorn and
pegasus, the chimera fits in the palm of Poki's hand. It lives
in the lagoon now, near the openings to the trods. Poki visits
it from time to time and they have become good friends. The
creature comes out of hiding at Poki's whistle. He tells it his
dreams as it glows softly in his hand, apparently listening.

Roleplaying Hints: You have seen hints here and

experienced events no one in your village has ever expe-

to experience those things as well. You plan to run away at
your earliest opportunity. You will stop at nothing, short
of harming one of the Menehune, to see the wide, wonder-
ful world that awaits you.

The Selkies

Just north of San Francisco, on the rocky coast of
Point Reyes, there exists a hidden freehold, down a misty
beach and up a steep cliff, in the living rock of the Earth.
It is inhabited by a motley of beautiful fae called selkies.

The seashores are their habitat, their refuge, their
home. Part human, part seal, selkies share a bond with the
creatures of the world's seashores. Their freeholds lie at the
edge of the great waters in hidden grottos and beach fronts.
The selkies of the Point Reyes freehold chose to build
theirs in a cave on the side of a cliff. They call themselves
the Selkies of Rocky Shore.

Selkies are skinchangers. When one changes to her
human form, she literally removes her skin and sets it
aside. The skin, which also has a mortal seeming, can
appear as a sealskin vest, coat or even a wetsuit. A selkie
who has lost her skin may not cast any faerie magic until
she retrieves it. The skin carries in it the selkie's Glamour
and her fae essence. The destruction of a selkie's skin is
disastrous to her. (See "Appendix," pg. 127-8, Immortal
Eyes: The Toybox for more information on the selkies.)
Both the selkies and the Menehune prize a resounding
respect fornature. Because of this and the long history they
share, a standing alliance has developed between the two
kith. Eitherofthem wouldrally to the other. The Menehune
lore-keeperseven believe the large-eyed, red-skinned build-
ers who preceded the current Menehune were related to

Immortal Cyes: Shadows on the hn

the selkies. The gentle seal folk do not contradict this
theory. As a matter of fact, they have remained suspi-
ciously silent on the subject.

Back in the days of the closing of Silver's Gate, the
selkies were given the task of delivering the Shadowstone
to the Menehune. They safeguarded it all the w:
Hawai'i, conveying it into the care of their allies. The
selkies have kept this knowledge sacred. They and only a
few others know the history of the Shadowstone.

Ondine

Ondine servesas leader of the Selkies of Rocky Shore.
She guides them with fairness and wisdom. Tall and
graceful, she looks the part of a gentle queen. However,
because the selkies do not adhere to a hierarchical social
structure, instead considering everyone completely equal,
her role is only titular. The others turn to her for guidance,
but she wields no authority over them.

Ondine is proud to be a selkie. She strongly believes in
the preservation of the selkie line and tradition. She estab-
lished her freehold at Rocky Shore in order to teach selkies
about their heritage and to encourage selkie solidarity.

When a selkie dies, her skin falls away from her. If the
selkie is a grump, prepared for death, she has already made
arrangements for the skin to be passed on to one of her kinain
or she has named an heir who will choose the next recipient
of her skin. Whichever kinain receives it will acquire some
knowledge of the selkies, but not necessarily an understand-
ing of the importance of the ancestral inheritanc

Sometimes other Kithain steal selkie skins for the
Glamour they hold, for the skin's resale value or for the
Kithain'sown perverse pleasure. Unseelie have been known


to kill a selkie for her skin. Ondine and her kithmates do
what they can to fight this horrible practice and retrieve
stolen skins.

Ondine and those who live with her keep a constant
vigil for selkies who are dying or who have died with no
heir. She has acquired enough of a reputation that many
seek her out. She accepts the skins of these selkies and
finds worthy kinain upon whom to bestow them. Then,
she teaches the chosen selkie fledge why he should be
proud to be a selkie and what he can do to strengthen the
selkie race.

Ondine has acquired Queen Aeron's trust and re-
spect. She keeps the queen informed about happenings on
the northern coastline and advises her on matters that
pertain to the selkies. The queen, understanding the
significance of a selkie death, notifies Ondine if she hears
of one. Many selkies live up and down the Pacific coast-
line, andalthough Ondine does not know them all, she has
established contacts, such as the queen, who keep her up-
to-date on their activities. Her good deeds are well-known
among the selkies.

Image: Ondine's lithe figure moves like seaweed,
smooth and supple. She wears loose-fitting shifts of
gauze in natural colors, the long sleeves reaching down
to her webbed fingers. Her thin face is sculpted in
symmetrical lines, with an upturned nose, high

Roleplaying Hints: You feel a great responsibility

and treachery directed at them, you have decided to
stand up and fight the perpetrators. Your kith has as
much a right to grow and prosper as any other. You
would die to protect a selkie skin, which in your mind
is the most valuable treasure a selkie may ever hold. In
your opinion, when you rescue a skin from the thieves
who stole it, you are freeing one of your brothers or
sisters from deadly kidnappers.

Thessalin

Ondine and Thessalin have been lovers for years.
Thessalin helps Ondine when she travels to retrieve a
stolen selkie skin. On more than one occasion, he has
saved her life.

A quiet, young wilder, Thessalin considers himself
not only Ondine's protector, but the protector of the
entire freehold. Sometimes he travels to distant places to
retrieve stolen skins, but he does it more out of a sense of
duty than a desire to travel. Actually, Thessalin hates
wandering far from Ondine. He fears that if he stays away
too long, she will find another lover.

A talented artist, Thessalin carves beautiful figures
from rock and coral. Among his best are seals and walrus,
dancing selkies and swimming dolphins. Thessalin never


keeps them for himself, but gives them to the childlings at
the freehold.

Thessalin came to the Point Reyes freehold on the
day he awoke to his faerie nature. He had been working as
agrunt on the docks in Seattle, loading crates onto a ship,
when Ondine noticed his kinain Glamour. She made an
excuse to meet him in her mortal guise, and the two fell in
love. On the night he asked her to marry him, under the
stars at Point Reyes, Ondine gave him a recently vacated
selkie skin, and Thessalin became one of them. Several
weeks later, once Thessalin had adjusted to his new self, he
moved into the freehold permanently, although Ondine
refused to take any sort of vow with him.

Orifcer

Nine-year-old Drifter lived in San Diego until only a

yearago. His father, a workaholic, served at the naval base,
and his mother had a job as a cashier at a woman's
department store. Drifter, then 8, stayed home alone a lot.
He had few friends. Always something of an outcast,
Drifter was bored. He had tired of reading and watching
TV. Computer games didn't thrill him anymore. He began
to feel an empty space growing inside him. Then Drifter
started hanging out at the beach. His parents strictly
forbade him to go there alone, but the water called to him,
kept his mind off his troubles. It filled the void inside him.


He loved to watch the waves roll in and the surfers...oh,

the surfers! Drifter watched them for hours on end, dream-

ing of the day when he too would be able to play in the surf.
Drifter's grandfather was a selkie. When the old

grump decided it was time for him to give up his skin, he

chose his grandson as his inheritor. He asked Ondine to

help him deliver the treasure to Drifter. When the time

came, Ondine guided the childling through it, teaching

him about himself and his new abilities. After awhile,

Drifter asked to move into the freehold where he could be

free to live as he had always dreamed. Ondine helped him

to break away gracefully from his parents, using her Glam-

our to ease their fears and to make them believe their son

was well and would be home to visit from time to time.

They chose to believe he was going away to a school for

children with special problems.

A lovely wilder selkie, Mouette has lived in the Point
Reyes freehold for nearly six years. She takes care of the
childlings who live there, entertaining them and making
sure they remain safe. She teaches them to find the best
seashells, string necklaces and carve coral. The childlings
would forget to eat and sleep at the right times ifshe didn't
prepare their meals and force them to a schedule.
Mouette came to the Point Reyes freehold with her
brother when she was 21. Her awakening came somewhat
abruptly and under tragic circumstances. Mouette had been
married for a year to a cold and angry man. She had a
promising future as an actress, and her husband served as her

manager. He pushed her career, his sights on the dollar signs
that would follow her success. When he found out she was
pregnant, her husband was furious. Then, when she refused
tohave an abortion, he became violent. He kicked herin the

soon as they let her out of the hospital, she walked down to
the beach with the intention of killing herself.

dresses in straight plain clothes, hiding her soft, voluptuous
body beneath heavy shifts and drab colors. Her hair has a
blue tint to its blackness that reminds one of the deepest
depths of the ocean. The rich purple of her eyes is given
intensity by the tiny black waves that swirl through it.

Roleplaying Hints: That day on the beach when you
became a selkie was both the happiest and the saddest day
of your life. You realized that your life had to go on without
your child. A whole new door was opening even as an ugly
old one was closing. You felt that here was an opportunity
you didn't really deserve, a chance to start over again. You
love the childlings you work with and would give your life
for them. For that matter, you love all the other selkies as
well, even Simon, although you wish he would shut up
about his darling, precious little girl.

CDorse

Morse had suspected his sister would try something
foolish once she was released from the hospital. Of course,
he thought it would be to kill her husband. He never
considered that she might try to kill herself.

Morse jumped in after her that night on the beach.
The sea's darkness made it impossible to find her, but he
wouldn't give up. His lungs burned with the effort, and
more than once he swallowed water. When at last he had
given up, he found that he no longer had the strength to
swim back. The sea took him in.

Like a flash of light it came; the sea lifted him up, and
he took a deep breath of air. Floating next to him, he saw
Mouette and, to his surprise, a seal. Mouette was uncon-
scious and, in his delirium, he could have sworn the seal
was supporting her. Then Morse too lost consciousness.

Morse awoke on the beach with Mouette in his arms
and that is how Ondine and Thessalin found them. Morse
er's acceptance of their situation alarming.
He instinctively trusted Ondine, but he was reticent to
throwaway hisentire life, a good job in computer program-

found his


ming, his apartment, everything, just to go and be with
them. He realized that he was no longer the same person,
but a part of him didn't want to move on. Mouette finally
convinced him.

Morse has a charming, fun-loving personality, but he
is prone to fits of depression that can last for days. Older
than his sister, he feels responsible for her and carries a
profound guilt at her loss. It tears him up that he cannot
find a solution to her sadness. He should have helped her
get away from that man long before anything so tragic
could happen.

Morse has idea after idea for changes to the freehold.
He wants to bring it into the twentieth century. Having
studied and worked with computers all his life, Morse
misses them greatly. He has been chipping away at Ondine
to allow him to set up a computer in the freehold. So far,
Ondine has resisted. She fears the Banality that the
machine will bring, despite Morse's reassurances to the
contrary.

Simon

Growing up in Charleston, South Carolina, Simon
led a relatively normal life. He married right after high
school and began work in the offshore construction busi-
ness. By the age of 20, Simon had a daughter, Stephanie,
and a drinking problem. His wife divorced him, and his
boss fired him. Simon started on a long downhill slide. Job
after job, Simon messed up and got fired. He did more one-
night stands than he could count. Then, early one morning

Chapcer Six: The Gallain and Prodigal


after an all-night drinking binge, Simon walked down to
the beach and passed out. He woke up in the county jail.
They cleaned him up and started him on rehabilitation.
Simon swore off alcohol and began attending support
group meetings.

Before long, Simon's health improved. He earned
Employee-of-the-Month at the local restaurant where he
waited tables. He even visited his daughter. Then he got
the news. His ex-wife was moving to San Francisco with
her new boyfriend. She would, of course, be taking
Stephanie with her. Simon experienced a severe setback.
He got drunker than he had ever been.

That night, Simon meta kindly old man seated at the
bar. The old man had the most compassionate smile and
eyes that Simon had ever seen. He ended up pouring his
heart out to the old man, telling him things and feelings he
had never expressed to anyone. The old man listened,
then suggested they walk down to the beach for some fresh
ait, It was a‘chilly night, and soon Simon was shivering,

The old man offered Simon his coat. Simon refused,
but the old man insisted. Then, just as Simon had given in
and was reaching for the coat, the old man held back.

"Young man," the fellow stated, "if give you my coat,
you have to promise me one thing."

"What?" Simon asked, intrigued by the old man's
sudden seriousness.

"You must promise me that you will smile and be happy."

Simon hesitated, unsure. Then, "Yeah. Okay, ol'
buddy. Whatever you say."

The old man gave Simon his coat and upon slipping
into it, Simon became Kithain. Later, he learned that the
old man, aselkie grump, had been looking fora worthy heir

to his skin. He had found it in Simon. Simon spent the
next few months at the selkie freehold in Charleston
learning about his new self.

never entirely exorcised.

Not until college did Diana discover her faerie nature.
Itcame as quite a shock to her, and she experienced a severe

what she was, she began to blossom physically. Her youthful
gangliness turned to healthy strength and beauty. Diana,
disgusted by her own form, turned to her studies as a means
of escape. She buried herself in her books, completing her
bachelor's and master's degrees within five years.

Diana studied and later taught anthropology at Bos-
ton University. Single-mindedly, she pursued knowledge
of how and why people acted the way they did. She delved


into cultural and racial differences, hoping to discover
some pattern or meaning in her own past. She didn't find
the answers, however, until she met Ryder. Over one
lunch conversation, he brought it all together for her. She

epted her curse and vowed to fight at his side against

the world. Herself-consciousness turned to fanatical anger
that had infected her, ruining her entire life.

Growing up in the dirty streets of Puerto Rico, Julio
Varg:
more murders and robberies than he could count. Ritual
